## Chapter: Chapter 5: Folding Algorithms for Polyhedra

### Introduction

In the previous chapters, we have explored the fascinating world of origami, linkages, and polyhedra. We have learned about the mathematical principles behind these structures and how they can be used to create intricate and beautiful designs. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the realm of polyhedra and explore the concept of folding algorithms.

Folding algorithms are mathematical procedures that describe how a polyhedron can be folded along its edges or faces. These algorithms are essential in the design and construction of origami models, as they provide a systematic approach to creating complex folds. They are also used in the field of computational geometry, where they are used to solve problems related to polyhedra.

In this chapter, we will discuss the basics of folding algorithms, including the different types of folds and how they can be represented mathematically. We will also explore some of the most commonly used folding algorithms, such as the edge-folding algorithm and the face-folding algorithm. These algorithms will be illustrated with examples and step-by-step instructions, making them accessible to both beginners and experienced origami enthusiasts.

Furthermore, we will discuss the applications of folding algorithms in the construction of origami models. We will learn how to use these algorithms to create complex folds and how to incorporate them into our origami designs. We will also explore the concept of duality in origami, where a fold on one side of a polyhedron corresponds to a fold on the other side.

Finally, we will discuss the limitations and challenges of folding algorithms, as well as potential future developments in this field. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of folding algorithms and their role in the world of origami and polyhedra. So let's dive in and discover the beauty and complexity of folding algorithms for polyhedra.




### Subsection: 4.5a Definition and Classification

In the previous sections, we have explored the concept of symmetry in polyhedra and its practical applications. In this section, we will delve into the classification of polyhedra, specifically focusing on regular and semi-regular polyhedra.

#### 4.5a.1 Regular Polyhedra

A regular polyhedron is a polyhedron in which all faces are congruent regular polygons, all vertices are the same distance from the center of the polyhedron, and all edges have the same length. The regular polyhedra are the tetrahedron, cube, octahedron, dodecahedron, and icosahedron. These polyhedra are named based on the number of faces, vertices, and edges they possess. For example, the tetrahedron has four faces, six vertices, and four edges.

#### 4.5a.2 Semi-Regular Polyhedra

A semi-regular polyhedron is a polyhedron in which all faces are regular polygons, but not all faces are congruent. In other words, the vertices of a semi-regular polyhedron are not all the same distance from the center of the polyhedron. The semi-regular polyhedra are the cuboctahedron, icosidodecahedron, and rhombicuboctahedron. These polyhedra are named based on the combination of regular polygons they possess. For example, the cuboctahedron has eight cubical faces and six octagonal faces.

#### 4.5a.3 Classification of Polyhedra

The classification of polyhedra is based on the number of faces, vertices, and edges they possess. The regular and semi-regular polyhedra are the simplest and most symmetrical polyhedra. They are followed by the Archimedean solids, which are polyhedra with regular polygon faces but irregular vertex arrangements. The remaining polyhedra are classified as non-Archimedean solids.

In the next section, we will explore the folding characteristics of these polyhedra and how they can be used in origami and linkages.




#### 4.5b Properties and Characteristics

In this section, we will explore the properties and characteristics of regular and semi-regular polyhedra. These properties are not only interesting from a mathematical perspective, but also have practical applications in various fields such as architecture, design, and computer graphics.

#### 4.5b.1 Regular Polyhedra

Regular polyhedra have several unique properties that set them apart from other polyhedra. One of the most notable properties is their symmetry. Each regular polyhedron has a symmetry group that describes all the symmetries of the polyhedron. For example, the tetrahedron has a symmetry group of order 12, the cube has a symmetry group of order 48, and the icosahedron has a symmetry group of order 60.

Another important property of regular polyhedra is their duality. The dual of a regular polyhedron is another regular polyhedron with the same number of vertices, edges, and faces, but with different types of polygons. For example, the dual of a tetrahedron is an octahedron, the dual of a cube is a 16-cell, and the dual of an icosahedron is a dodecahedron.

#### 4.5b.2 Semi-Regular Polyhedra

Semi-regular polyhedra also have interesting properties that make them unique. One of the most notable properties is their semi-regularity. This means that while all faces are regular polygons, not all vertices are the same distance from the center of the polyhedron. This results in a more complex symmetry group compared to regular polyhedra. For example, the cuboctahedron has a symmetry group of order 48, the icosidodecahedron has a symmetry group of order 120, and the rhombicuboctahedron has a symmetry group of order 96.

Another important property of semi-regular polyhedra is their duality. The dual of a semi-regular polyhedron is another semi-regular polyhedron with the same number of vertices, edges, and faces, but with different types of polygons. For example, the dual of a cuboctahedron is an icosidodecahedron, the dual of an icosidodecahedron is a cuboctahedron, and the dual of a rhombicuboctahedron is a rhombicuboctahedron.

#### 4.5b.3 Applications in Origami

The properties and characteristics of regular and semi-regular polyhedra have practical applications in origami. Origami is the art of paper folding, and it is often used to create three-dimensional shapes. The symmetry and duality of regular and semi-regular polyhedra make them ideal shapes for origami, as they can be easily folded from a single sheet of paper.

For example, the tetrahedron, cube, and icosahedron are commonly used in origami due to their symmetry and ease of construction. The tetrahedron can be folded from a square piece of paper, the cube from a rectangular piece of paper, and the icosahedron from a pentagonal piece of paper. The duality of these polyhedra also allows for the creation of more complex origami shapes.

In addition, the semi-regular polyhedra have also been used in origami, particularly the cuboctahedron and icosidodecahedron. These polyhedra have a more complex symmetry compared to regular polyhedra, making them more challenging to fold but also more visually interesting.

#### 4.5b.4 Applications in Computer Graphics

The properties and characteristics of regular and semi-regular polyhedra also have applications in computer graphics. In particular, the symmetry and duality of these polyhedra are used in the creation of 3D models and textures.

For example, the symmetry of regular polyhedra is used to create textures that repeat seamlessly in all directions. This is particularly useful in computer games and other applications where textures need to be repeated over large surfaces.

The duality of regular and semi-regular polyhedra is also used in the creation of 3D models. By using the dual of a polyhedron, complex 3D shapes can be created from simple polygons, making it easier to model and manipulate objects in computer graphics.

In conclusion, the properties and characteristics of regular and semi-regular polyhedra have practical applications in various fields, including origami and computer graphics. Understanding these properties is essential for anyone working with these shapes, whether it be in art, design, or computer science.





#### 4.5c Practical Examples

In this section, we will explore some practical examples of regular and semi-regular polyhedra. These examples will demonstrate the applications of these polyhedra in various fields.

#### 4.5c.1 Regular Polyhedra in Architecture

Regular polyhedra have been used in architecture for centuries. The most common example is the cube, which is used in the design of buildings and structures. The cube's symmetry and simplicity make it a popular choice for architects. Other regular polyhedra, such as the tetrahedron and icosahedron, have also been used in architectural design, particularly in the construction of domes and other geometric structures.

#### 4.5c.2 Semi-Regular Polyhedra in Computer Graphics

Semi-regular polyhedra have found applications in computer graphics, particularly in the creation of 3D models. The cuboctahedron, with its combination of cubes and octahedrons, is often used as a base for creating more complex 3D shapes. The icosidodecahedron, with its combination of icosahedrons and dodecahedrons, is also used in computer graphics. These polyhedra are particularly useful for creating smooth and visually appealing surfaces.

#### 4.5c.3 Regular and Semi-Regular Polyhedra in Origami

Origami, the art of paper folding, often involves the creation of polyhedra. Regular and semi-regular polyhedra are particularly popular in origami due to their symmetry and simplicity. The cube, tetrahedron, and icosahedron are all commonly folded in origami, as are the cuboctahedron and icosidodecahedron. These polyhedra are not only visually appealing, but also provide a challenge for origami artists to create.

#### 4.5c.4 Regular and Semi-Regular Polyhedra in Mathematics

Regular and semi-regular polyhedra have been studied extensively in mathematics, particularly in the fields of geometry and topology. The study of these polyhedra has led to the development of important mathematical concepts, such as symmetry groups and duality. These concepts have applications in various fields, including computer science and engineering.

In conclusion, regular and semi-regular polyhedra have a wide range of applications in various fields. Their unique properties and characteristics make them valuable tools for architects, computer graphics artists, origami artists, and mathematicians. As we continue to explore the world of polyhedra, we will discover even more practical examples of these fascinating geometric shapes.

We will then delve into the mathematical principles behind these algorithms, including the concept of crease patterns and the role of symmetry in origami design. We will also explore the different types of folding techniques, such as valley folds and mountain folds, and how they are used in geometric folding algorithms.

Next, we will introduce the concept of linkages, which are interconnected chains of rigid bodies that can move and transform in a controlled manner. We will discuss how linkages are used in geometric folding algorithms to create intricate and dynamic structures. We will also explore the concept of kinematic chains, which are used to analyze the movement of linkages and how they relate to geometric folding algorithms.

Finally, we will delve into the world of polyhedra, which are three-dimensional shapes with flat faces. We will discuss how polyhedra are used in geometric folding algorithms and how they can be folded and transformed using various techniques. So let's dive in and discover the world of geometric folding algorithms!


## Chapter 5: Linkages and Their Folding Characteristics:




### Subsection: 4.6a Definition and Classification

In the previous section, we explored the properties and applications of regular and semi-regular polyhedra. In this section, we will delve deeper into the classification of polyhedra, specifically focusing on convex and non-convex polyhedra.

#### 4.6a.1 Convex Polyhedra

A convex polyhedron is a polyhedron in which all interior angles are less than 180 degrees. This means that any line segment connecting two points within the polyhedron will lie entirely within the polyhedron. Convex polyhedra are important in geometry and have many practical applications, particularly in architecture and engineering.

#### 4.6a.2 Non-Convex Polyhedra

Non-convex polyhedra, on the other hand, have at least one interior angle greater than 180 degrees. This means that there exists a line segment connecting two points within the polyhedron that does not lie entirely within the polyhedron. Non-convex polyhedra are less common than convex polyhedra, but they still have important applications, particularly in computer graphics and origami.

#### 4.6a.3 Classification of Polyhedra

Polyhedra can be classified into several categories based on their properties. In addition to convex and non-convex polyhedra, we can also classify polyhedra based on their symmetry, regularity, and the number of faces, edges, and vertices they have. This classification system allows us to better understand and analyze different types of polyhedra.

#### 4.6a.4 Convex and Non-Convex Polyhedra in Origami

Origami, the art of paper folding, often involves the creation of polyhedra. Convex and non-convex polyhedra are both used in origami, with convex polyhedra being more common due to their simplicity and ease of construction. The classification of polyhedra is particularly important in origami, as it allows origami artists to create more complex and visually appealing designs.

#### 4.6a.5 Convex and Non-Convex Polyhedra in Architecture

In architecture, both convex and non-convex polyhedra are used. Convex polyhedra, with their simple and symmetrical shapes, are often used in the design of buildings and structures. Non-convex polyhedra, on the other hand, are used in more complex and innovative architectural designs. The classification of polyhedra is crucial in architecture, as it allows architects to create unique and visually striking designs.

#### 4.6a.6 Convex and Non-Convex Polyhedra in Computer Graphics

In computer graphics, both convex and non-convex polyhedra are used. Convex polyhedra are often used in 3D modeling and rendering, due to their simplicity and ease of manipulation. Non-convex polyhedra, on the other hand, are used in more complex and realistic 3D models. The classification of polyhedra is essential in computer graphics, as it allows for the creation of more accurate and visually appealing 3D models.

#### 4.6a.7 Convex and Non-Convex Polyhedra in Mathematics

In mathematics, both convex and non-convex polyhedra are studied. Convex polyhedra are particularly important in geometry, as they have many interesting properties and can be used to prove important theorems. Non-convex polyhedra, while less studied, still have important applications in topology and other branches of mathematics. The classification of polyhedra is crucial in mathematics, as it allows for a deeper understanding of these geometric objects.


## Chapter 4: Polyhedra and Their Folding Characteristics:




### Subsection: 4.6b Properties and Characteristics

In this section, we will explore the properties and characteristics of convex and non-convex polyhedra. These properties are important in understanding the behavior of polyhedra and their applications in various fields.

#### 4.6b.1 Convex Polyhedra Properties

Convex polyhedra have several important properties that make them useful in various applications. These include:

- All interior angles are less than 180 degrees.
- Any line segment connecting two points within the polyhedron lies entirely within the polyhedron.
- The volume of a convex polyhedron can be calculated using the formula: $$V = \frac{1}{3}Ah$$ where $A$ is the area of the base and $h$ is the height of the polyhedron.
- Convex polyhedra have a simple and regular structure, making them easy to analyze and construct.

#### 4.6b.2 Non-Convex Polyhedra Properties

Non-convex polyhedra also have important properties that make them useful in various applications. These include:

- At least one interior angle is greater than 180 degrees.
- There exists a line segment connecting two points within the polyhedron that does not lie entirely within the polyhedron.
- The volume of a non-convex polyhedron can be calculated using more complex methods, such as the Brunn-Minkowski theorem.
- Non-convex polyhedra have a more complex and irregular structure, making them more challenging to analyze and construct.

#### 4.6b.3 Comparison of Convex and Non-Convex Polyhedra

While convex and non-convex polyhedra have different properties, they both have important applications in various fields. Convex polyhedra are often used in architecture and engineering due to their simple and regular structure, while non-convex polyhedra are used in computer graphics and origami due to their more complex and irregular structure.

In the next section, we will explore the folding characteristics of convex and non-convex polyhedra, and how these properties play a role in their folding behavior.

We have learned about the different types of polyhedra, their properties, and how they can be folded. We have also discussed the importance of understanding the folding characteristics of polyhedra in various applications, such as origami and linkages.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the concept of vertex folding, which allows us to create complex polyhedra by folding along the edges of simpler polyhedra. This technique is not only useful for creating intricate designs, but it also helps us understand the underlying structure and symmetry of polyhedra.

Another important aspect of polyhedra is their duality, where a polyhedron and its dual have similar folding characteristics. This concept is crucial in understanding the behavior of polyhedra and their folding patterns.

Overall, the study of polyhedra and their folding characteristics is a vast and ever-evolving field. By understanding the principles and techniques discussed in this chapter, we can continue to explore and create new and exciting designs.

This technique is not only useful for creating intricate designs, but it also helps us understand the underlying structure and symmetry of polyhedra.

Another important aspect of polyhedra is their duality, where a polyhedron and its dual have similar folding characteristics. This concept is crucial in understanding the behavior of polyhedra and their folding patterns.

Overall, the study of polyhedra and their folding characteristics is a vast and ever-evolving field. By understanding the principles and techniques discussed in this chapter, we can continue to explore and create new and exciting designs.

## Chapter: Geometric Folding Algorithms: Linkages, Origami, Polyhedra":

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the fascinating world of geometric folding algorithms. These algorithms are used to create complex designs and structures by folding simple shapes. This technique has been used in various fields, including origami, linkages, and polyhedra. By understanding the principles behind these algorithms, we can create intricate and beautiful designs that were previously thought to be impossible.

We will begin by discussing the basics of geometric folding, including the concept of creases and how they are used to create folds. We will then delve into the different types of geometric folding algorithms, such as the Miura-ori and the Kawasaki-ori. These algorithms are based on the principles of origami, and they allow us to create complex designs by folding simple shapes.

Next, we will explore the use of geometric folding algorithms in linkages. Linkages are mechanical structures that are used to connect multiple objects together. By using geometric folding algorithms, we can create intricate and efficient linkages that can perform a variety of tasks.

Finally, we will discuss the application of geometric folding algorithms in polyhedra. Polyhedra are three-dimensional shapes that are made up of flat surfaces. By using geometric folding algorithms, we can create complex polyhedra that have intricate and beautiful designs.

Throughout this chapter, we will also discuss the mathematical principles behind these algorithms and how they are used to create these designs. By the end of this chapter, you will have a deeper understanding of geometric folding algorithms and how they can be used to create beautiful and complex designs. So let's dive in and explore the world of geometric folding algorithms.


## Chapter 5: Geometric Folding Algorithms: Linkages, Origami, Polyhedra":




### Subsection: 4.6c Practical Examples

In this section, we will explore some practical examples of convex and non-convex polyhedra and their applications. These examples will help us better understand the properties and characteristics of these polyhedra and how they are used in various fields.

#### 4.6c.1 Convex Polyhedra Examples

Convex polyhedra have a wide range of applications in various fields. One of the most common applications is in architecture and engineering. The simple and regular structure of convex polyhedra makes them ideal for constructing buildings and structures. For example, the Green D.4, a type of convex polyhedron, is commonly used in the construction of domes and vaults.

Another important application of convex polyhedra is in computer graphics. The simple and regular structure of these polyhedra makes them easy to represent and manipulate in computer programs. This is why convex polyhedra are often used in 3D modeling and animation.

#### 4.6c.2 Non-Convex Polyhedra Examples

Non-convex polyhedra also have important applications in various fields. One of the most common applications is in origami, the art of paper folding. The more complex and irregular structure of non-convex polyhedra makes them challenging to construct, but also allows for more intricate and detailed designs.

Non-convex polyhedra are also used in computer graphics, particularly in the field of computer-aided design (CAD). The more complex and irregular structure of these polyhedra allows for more precise and detailed designs, making them useful in creating complex 3D models.

#### 4.6c.3 Comparison of Convex and Non-Convex Polyhedra Examples

While convex and non-convex polyhedra have different applications, they both play important roles in various fields. Convex polyhedra are often used in construction and engineering, while non-convex polyhedra are used in origami and computer graphics. The properties and characteristics of these polyhedra make them useful in their respective applications.

In the next section, we will explore the folding characteristics of convex and non-convex polyhedra, and how these properties play a role in their folding behavior.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the concept of duality in polyhedra. We have seen how the dual of a polyhedron is a polyhedron with a different set of vertices, edges, and faces. This concept is crucial in understanding the folding behavior of polyhedra, as it allows us to map the folding of one polyhedron to the unfolding of its dual.

Another important aspect of polyhedra is their symmetry. We have seen how the symmetry of a polyhedron can affect its folding characteristics. By understanding the symmetry of a polyhedron, we can predict how it will fold and make necessary adjustments to achieve a desired folding pattern.

Overall, this chapter has provided a comprehensive guide to understanding the folding characteristics of polyhedra. By studying the different types of polyhedra and their properties, we can gain a deeper understanding of their folding behavior and apply this knowledge in various applications.

## Chapter: Geometric Folding Algorithms: Linkages, Origami, Polyhedra

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the fascinating world of polyhedra and their properties. Polyhedra are three-dimensional geometric objects that are bounded by flat polygonal surfaces. They are an essential part of mathematics, with applications in various fields such as architecture, engineering, and computer graphics. Understanding the properties of polyhedra is crucial for designing and constructing complex structures, as well as for creating realistic 3D models in computer graphics.

We will begin by discussing the basics of polyhedra, including their definition, types, and properties. We will then delve into the concept of duality in polyhedra, which is a fundamental concept in geometry. Duality refers to the relationship between a polyhedron and its dual, where the vertices, edges, and faces of the polyhedron are mapped to the edges, vertices, and faces of its dual. This concept is essential for understanding the symmetry and balance of polyhedra.

Next, we will explore the concept of folding in polyhedra. Folding is a process of transforming a polyhedron into a simpler form by collapsing its faces. This process is closely related to the concept of duality, as the folded polyhedron can be seen as the dual of its original form. We will discuss different types of folds, such as edge folds, vertex folds, and face folds, and how they can be used to simplify complex polyhedra.

Finally, we will touch upon the applications of polyhedra and their properties in various fields. We will see how polyhedra are used in architecture to create visually appealing and structurally sound buildings. We will also explore how polyhedra are used in engineering for designing and analyzing complex structures. Additionally, we will discuss how polyhedra are used in computer graphics to create realistic 3D models and animations.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of polyhedra and their properties, as well as their applications in various fields. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for the rest of the book, where we will delve deeper into the world of geometric folding algorithms and their applications. So, let's begin our journey into the fascinating world of polyhedra and their properties.

### Section: 5.1 Computational Complexity of Folding Algorithms:

In this section, we will explore the computational complexity of folding algorithms. As we have seen in the previous section, these algorithms are used to create complex geometric structures by folding simple sheets of paper or other materials. However, the process of folding can be computationally intensive, especially for more complex structures.

#### 5.1a Basic Concepts

Before delving into the computational complexity of folding algorithms, let's first review some basic concepts. A folding algorithm is a set of instructions that tell us how to fold a sheet of paper or other material to create a specific geometric structure. These algorithms are often based on the concept of linkages, where the folds are connected to each other in a specific way to create a stable structure.

One of the key factors that contribute to the computational complexity of folding algorithms is the number of folds required to create a structure. For example, a simple origami crane can be created with just a few folds, while a more complex structure may require hundreds or even thousands of folds. This means that the time and resources required to create the structure also increase with the number of folds.

Another factor that contributes to the computational complexity is the type of folds used in the algorithm. Some folds, such as mountain and valley folds, are relatively simple and can be easily calculated. However, more complex folds, such as pleats and accordion folds, may require more complex calculations and can increase the computational complexity of the algorithm.

In addition to the number of folds and type of folds, the computational complexity of folding algorithms is also affected by the size and shape of the structure being created. Larger and more complex structures may require more calculations and resources, making them more computationally intensive to create.

To better understand the computational complexity of folding algorithms, we can use the concept of time complexity. Time complexity is a measure of how long an algorithm takes to run, and it is often expressed in terms of the input size. In the case of folding algorithms, the input size is the number of folds required to create the structure. Using time complexity, we can classify folding algorithms as either polynomial or exponential.

Polynomial time complexity means that the algorithm runs in a finite amount of time, regardless of the size of the input. This is desirable for folding algorithms, as it allows us to create complex structures in a reasonable amount of time. However, some folding algorithms may have exponential time complexity, meaning that the time required to run the algorithm increases exponentially with the size of the input. This can make it impractical to create larger and more complex structures.

In conclusion, the computational complexity of folding algorithms is influenced by various factors, including the number of folds, type of folds, and size and shape of the structure. By understanding these factors and using concepts such as time complexity, we can better appreciate the complexity of these algorithms and their applications in computational geometry.

### Section: 5.1c Practical Applications

In this section, we will explore some practical applications of the computational complexity of folding algorithms. These applications demonstrate the importance of understanding the computational complexity of folding algorithms in real-world scenarios.

#### 5.1c.1 Folding Algorithms in Origami Design

Origami, the art of paper folding, has been practiced for centuries and has been used to create a wide range of objects, from simple toys to complex sculptures. The design of these objects often involves the use of folding algorithms, which are used to determine the sequence of folds required to create the desired shape.

The computational complexity of these algorithms is a crucial factor in the design process. For example, consider the design of a simple origami bird. The design process involves determining the sequence of folds required to create the bird's wings, body, and head. This process can be represented as a graph, where each vertex represents a fold and each edge represents a sequence of folds.

The complexity of this graph can be calculated using the algorithms discussed in the previous section. For example, the breadth-first search algorithm can be used to find the shortest path from the root vertex (representing the initial state of the paper) to the vertices representing the final state of the bird. The depth-first search algorithm can be used to find the longest path, which can be used to determine the maximum number of folds required to create the bird.

#### 5.1c.2 Folding Algorithms in Polyhedra Construction

Polyhedra, geometric objects with flat faces, are another area where folding algorithms are used. The construction of polyhedra often involves the use of nets, which are flat representations of the polyhedron. The process of constructing a polyhedron from a net involves folding the net along the lines representing the edges of the polyhedron.

The computational complexity of this process is similar to that of origami design. The sequence of folds required to construct the polyhedron can be represented as a graph, and the complexity of this graph can be calculated using the algorithms discussed in the previous section.

#### 5.1c.3 Folding Algorithms in Robotics

Folding algorithms are also used in robotics, particularly in the design of robots that can transform from one shape to another. These transformations often involve the use of folding algorithms, which are used to determine the sequence of movements required to transform the robot from one shape to another.

The computational complexity of these algorithms is a crucial factor in the design process. For example, consider the design of a robot that can transform from a flat sheet to a cube. The design process involves determining the sequence of movements required to create the cube. This process can be represented as a graph, where each vertex represents a movement and each edge represents a sequence of movements.

The complexity of this graph can be calculated using the algorithms discussed in the previous section. For example, the breadth-first search algorithm can be used to find the shortest path from the root vertex (representing the initial state of the robot) to the vertices representing the final state of the cube. The depth-first search algorithm can be used to find the longest path, which can be used to determine the maximum number of movements required to create the cube.

In conclusion, the computational complexity of folding algorithms plays a crucial role in a wide range of practical applications, from origami design to polyhedra construction to robotics. Understanding this complexity is essential for designing efficient and effective algorithms for these applications.




### Subsection: 5.2a Basic Concepts

In this section, we will introduce some basic concepts related to geometric data structures. These concepts are essential for understanding the algorithms and techniques used in the field of computational geometry.

#### 5.2a.1 Geometric Data Structures

A geometric data structure is a data structure that represents geometric objects, such as points, lines, and polygons, in a computer. These structures are used to store and manipulate geometric data efficiently. They are crucial for many applications, including computer graphics, computer-aided design, and robotics.

Geometric data structures can be broadly classified into two categories: implicit and explicit. Implicit data structures represent geometric objects implicitly, without explicitly storing all the points that make up the object. Explicit data structures, on the other hand, store all the points explicitly.

#### 5.2a.2 Implicit Data Structures

Implicit data structures are used to represent geometric objects in a compact and efficient manner. They are particularly useful for representing objects with complex shapes or large numbers of points.

One common type of implicit data structure is the implicit k-d tree. An implicit k-d tree is a variant of the k-d tree data structure that is used to represent high-dimensional data. It is particularly useful for representing data in a space with more than three dimensions.

Another type of implicit data structure is the implicit octree. An implicit octree is a variant of the octree data structure that is used to represent high-dimensional data. It is particularly useful for representing data in a space with more than three dimensions.

#### 5.2a.3 Explicit Data Structures

Explicit data structures are used to represent geometric objects by storing all the points that make up the object explicitly. They are particularly useful for representing objects with simple shapes or small numbers of points.

One common type of explicit data structure is the explicit k-d tree. An explicit k-d tree is a variant of the k-d tree data structure that is used to represent low-dimensional data. It is particularly useful for representing data in a space with three or fewer dimensions.

Another type of explicit data structure is the explicit octree. An explicit octree is a variant of the octree data structure that is used to represent low-dimensional data. It is particularly useful for representing data in a space with three or fewer dimensions.

#### 5.2a.4 Data Structure Complexity

The complexity of a data structure refers to the amount of space and time required to store and manipulate the data. In the context of geometric data structures, the complexity is often measured in terms of the number of points or the dimensionality of the data.

For example, an implicit k-d tree may have a complexity of O(n), where n is the number of points in the data set. On the other hand, an explicit k-d tree may have a complexity of O(log(n)), where log(n) is the logarithm of the number of points.

Understanding the complexity of a data structure is crucial for choosing the right data structure for a given application. It also helps in designing efficient algorithms for manipulating the data.

#### 5.2a.5 Data Structure Design

The design of a geometric data structure involves choosing the right data structure for a given application. This involves understanding the characteristics of the data, such as its size, shape, and dimensionality, and choosing a data structure that can represent the data efficiently.

For example, if the data is high-dimensional and complex, an implicit data structure may be more suitable. On the other hand, if the data is low-dimensional and simple, an explicit data structure may be more suitable.

The design of a data structure also involves considering the operations that need to be performed on the data. For example, if the data needs to be sorted, a data structure that supports efficient sorting, such as a k-d tree, may be more suitable.

In conclusion, understanding the basic concepts of geometric data structures is crucial for understanding the algorithms and techniques used in the field of computational geometry. These concepts form the foundation for the more advanced topics covered in the rest of this chapter.





### Subsection: 5.2b Data Structure Design

In the previous section, we introduced the concept of geometric data structures and discussed two types of implicit data structures. In this section, we will delve deeper into the design of these data structures, focusing on the implicit k-d tree and implicit octree.

#### 5.2b.1 Implicit k-d Tree

The implicit k-d tree is a variant of the k-d tree data structure that is used to represent high-dimensional data. It is particularly useful for representing data in a space with more than three dimensions.

The implicit k-d tree is designed to efficiently represent high-dimensional data by dividing the data space into smaller regions. Each node in the tree represents a region in the data space, and the tree is constructed by recursively dividing the data space along the dimensions.

The implicit k-d tree is an implicit data structure, meaning that it does not explicitly store all the points that make up the data. Instead, it stores a bounding box for each region in the data space, and uses this bounding box to determine whether a point falls within the region.

#### 5.2b.2 Implicit Octree

The implicit octree is another type of implicit data structure that is used to represent high-dimensional data. It is particularly useful for representing data in a space with more than three dimensions.

Similar to the implicit k-d tree, the implicit octree is designed to efficiently represent high-dimensional data by dividing the data space into smaller regions. Each node in the tree represents a region in the data space, and the tree is constructed by recursively dividing the data space along the dimensions.

The implicit octree is also an implicit data structure, meaning that it does not explicitly store all the points that make up the data. Instead, it stores a bounding box for each region in the data space, and uses this bounding box to determine whether a point falls within the region.

#### 5.2b.3 Data Structure Design Considerations

When designing a geometric data structure, there are several factors to consider. These include the dimensionality of the data, the complexity of the data, and the operations that need to be performed on the data.

For high-dimensional data, implicit data structures like the implicit k-d tree and implicit octree are often the most efficient. These data structures can efficiently represent the data and perform operations like point lookup and nearest neighbor search.

For complex data, explicit data structures like the explicit k-d tree and explicit octree may be more suitable. These data structures can store all the points that make up the data, allowing for more complex operations to be performed.

Finally, the operations that need to be performed on the data should also be considered. For example, if the main operation is point lookup, an implicit data structure may be more efficient. However, if the main operation is data insertion, an explicit data structure may be more suitable.

In the next section, we will discuss some of the operations that can be performed on geometric data structures, and how these operations can be efficiently implemented.





### Subsection: 5.2c Practical Applications

In this section, we will explore some practical applications of geometric data structures in the field of computational geometry. These applications demonstrate the power and versatility of these data structures in solving complex geometric problems.

#### 5.2c.1 Folding Algorithms

Folding algorithms are a class of geometric algorithms that are used to fold a piece of paper or a sheet of metal into a desired shape. These algorithms often involve the use of geometric data structures to represent the folding process.

For example, consider the problem of folding a piece of paper into a crane. This can be represented as a series of folds, each of which involves folding the paper along a specific line. The geometric data structure used to represent this problem could be an implicit k-d tree, where each node represents a region of the paper and the bounding box represents the area that will be folded.

The folding algorithm then involves recursively dividing the paper along the lines specified by the implicit k-d tree, until the desired shape is achieved.

#### 5.2c.2 Origami

Origami is the art of paper folding, and it involves the creation of complex shapes from a single sheet of paper. The design of these shapes often involves the use of geometric data structures, particularly the implicit k-d tree and the implicit octree.

For example, consider the design of a crane. This can be represented as a series of folds, each of which involves folding the paper along a specific line. The geometric data structure used to represent this problem could be an implicit k-d tree, where each node represents a region of the paper and the bounding box represents the area that will be folded.

The design algorithm then involves recursively dividing the paper along the lines specified by the implicit k-d tree, until the desired shape is achieved.

#### 5.2c.3 Polyhedra

Polyhedra are geometric objects that are bounded by flat polygonal surfaces. The design of these objects often involves the use of geometric data structures, particularly the implicit k-d tree and the implicit octree.

For example, consider the design of a cube. This can be represented as a series of faces, each of which is a square. The geometric data structure used to represent this problem could be an implicit k-d tree, where each node represents a face of the cube and the bounding box represents the area that will be folded.

The design algorithm then involves recursively dividing the cube along the lines specified by the implicit k-d tree, until the desired shape is achieved.

In conclusion, geometric data structures play a crucial role in the design and implementation of geometric algorithms. They provide a powerful and versatile tool for representing and manipulating geometric objects, and their practical applications are vast and varied.

## Chapter: Chapter 6: Folding Algorithms and Origami

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of folding algorithms and origami, two intertwined concepts that have captivated mathematicians and artists alike. Folding algorithms are computational methods used to create complex structures by folding a flat sheet of paper or other material. Origami, on the other hand, is the traditional Japanese art of paper folding, which has been elevated to a mathematical discipline.

The intersection of these two fields is a rich and complex one, with applications ranging from the design of complex structures in architecture and engineering, to the creation of intricate origami models. This chapter will explore the principles behind these algorithms, their applications, and the mathematical theories that underpin them.

We will begin by introducing the basic concepts of origami, including the fundamental folds and the principles of origami design. We will then move on to discuss folding algorithms, exploring how these computational methods can be used to create complex structures from simple folds. We will also discuss the role of symmetry in these algorithms, and how it can be used to create beautiful and intricate designs.

Throughout the chapter, we will use mathematical notation to describe these concepts. For example, we might represent a fold as a function $f(x)$, where $x$ is the point on the paper to be folded. We will also use geometric and topological concepts, such as the concept of a crease pattern, which is a geometric representation of the folds in a paper model.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the principles behind folding algorithms and origami, and you will be equipped with the knowledge to create your own complex origami models using computational methods. Whether you are a mathematician, an artist, or simply a curious reader, we hope that this chapter will spark your interest in this fascinating field.




### Subsection: 5.3a Basic Concepts

In this section, we will introduce some basic concepts that are essential for understanding efficient algorithms for folding. These concepts include the implicit k-d tree, the implicit octree, and the concept of recursive division.

#### 5.3a.1 Implicit k-d Tree

An implicit k-d tree is a geometric data structure that is used to represent a high-dimensional space. It is a generalization of the k-d tree, which is used to represent a two-dimensional space. The implicit k-d tree is particularly useful for representing the folding process in origami and paper folding algorithms.

In the context of origami, the implicit k-d tree can be used to represent the paper as a series of regions, each of which is represented by a node in the tree. The bounding box of each node represents the area that will be folded. The implicit k-d tree can then be used to recursively divide the paper along the lines specified by the tree, until the desired shape is achieved.

#### 5.3a.2 Implicit Octree

An implicit octree is another geometric data structure that is used to represent a high-dimensional space. It is a generalization of the octree, which is used to represent a three-dimensional space. The implicit octree is particularly useful for representing the folding process in polyhedra and other complex geometric shapes.

In the context of polyhedra, the implicit octree can be used to represent the polyhedron as a series of regions, each of which is represented by a node in the tree. The bounding box of each node represents the area that will be folded. The implicit octree can then be used to recursively divide the polyhedron along the lines specified by the tree, until the desired shape is achieved.

#### 5.3a.3 Recursive Division

Recursive division is a fundamental concept in the design of efficient algorithms for folding. It involves dividing a problem into smaller, more manageable subproblems, and then solving these subproblems recursively. This approach is particularly useful for problems that can be decomposed into a series of smaller, independent subproblems.

In the context of folding, recursive division can be used to solve complex folding problems by breaking them down into a series of simpler folding subproblems. This approach can greatly simplify the design of folding algorithms, and can also lead to more efficient solutions.

In the next section, we will explore some specific examples of efficient algorithms for folding, and discuss how these algorithms make use of the concepts introduced in this section.




### Subsection: 5.3b Algorithm Design

In this section, we will delve deeper into the design of efficient algorithms for folding. We will explore the concept of recursive division in more detail and discuss how it can be applied to the design of folding algorithms. We will also introduce the concept of implicit data structures and how they can be used to improve the efficiency of these algorithms.

#### 5.3b.1 Recursive Division in Folding Algorithms

Recursive division is a powerful tool in the design of folding algorithms. It allows us to break down a complex folding problem into a series of simpler subproblems, each of which can be solved recursively. This approach can greatly simplify the design of the algorithm and can lead to significant improvements in efficiency.

Consider, for example, the problem of folding a polyhedron. This problem can be broken down into a series of subproblems, each of which involves folding a smaller portion of the polyhedron. By solving each subproblem recursively, we can eventually fold the entire polyhedron.

#### 5.3b.2 Implicit Data Structures in Folding Algorithms

Implicit data structures can also play a crucial role in the design of folding algorithms. These data structures allow us to represent complex geometric shapes in a compact and efficient manner. This can be particularly useful in the context of folding, where we often need to represent and manipulate large and complex shapes.

Consider, for example, the implicit k-d tree and implicit octree, which we introduced in the previous section. These data structures allow us to represent a high-dimensional space in a compact and efficient manner. This can be particularly useful in the context of folding, where we often need to represent and manipulate large and complex shapes.

#### 5.3b.3 Complexity of Folding Algorithms

The complexity of a folding algorithm refers to the amount of time and space required to solve the algorithm. In the context of folding, the complexity can be influenced by a variety of factors, including the size and complexity of the shape being folded, the number of folds required, and the efficiency of the algorithm.

Consider, for example, the complexity of the Remez algorithm, a popular algorithm for finding the best approximation of a function. The complexity of this algorithm is influenced by the number of iterations required to find the best approximation, which in turn depends on the number of folds required and the efficiency of the algorithm.

In the next section, we will explore some specific examples of efficient algorithms for folding and discuss how they can be applied to solve real-world problems.




### Subsection: 5.3c Practical Applications

#### 5.3c.1 Folding in Robotics

One of the most promising applications of folding algorithms is in the field of robotics. Folding algorithms can be used to design and control the movement of robots, allowing them to perform complex tasks such as grasping and manipulating objects.

Consider, for example, the problem of designing a robot arm that can pick up and place objects. #### 5.3c.2 Folding in Architecture

Folding algorithms also have applications in the field of architecture. They can be used to design and construct complex architectural structures, such as bridges and buildings.

Consider, for example, the problem of designing a bridge. This problem can be formulated as a folding problem, where the bridge is represented as a polyhedron and the task of constructing the bridge is represented as a series of folds. By using the efficient algorithms discussed in this chapter, we can design a bridge that is strong, efficient, and aesthetically pleasing.

#### 5.3c.3 Folding in Computational Geometry

Finally, folding algorithms have applications in the field of computational geometry. They can be used to solve complex geometric problems, such as finding the intersection of two lines or the shortest path between two points.

Consider, for example, the problem of finding the shortest path between two points in a plane. This problem can be formulated as a folding problem, where the plane is represented as a polyhedron and the task of finding the shortest path is represented as a series of folds. By using the efficient algorithms discussed in this chapter, we can find the shortest path efficiently and accurately.

In conclusion, the efficient algorithms for folding discussed in this chapter have a wide range of practical applications in various fields. They allow us to solve complex problems in a simple and efficient manner, making them a powerful tool in the field of computational geometry.

### Subsection: 5.4a Basic Concepts

#### 5.4a.1 Discrete Differential Geometry

Discrete differential geometry is a branch of mathematics that deals with the study of geometric objects and their properties using discrete methods. It is particularly useful in the context of folding algorithms, as it allows us to represent and manipulate geometric objects in a discrete and computable manner.

#### 5.4a.2 Folding and Discrete Differential Geometry

Folding algorithms are a type of discrete differential geometry that deals with the problem of folding a geometric object along a given set of lines or curves. This is a fundamental problem in computational geometry, with applications in robotics, architecture, and computational geometry.

#### 5.4a.3 Folding Algorithms and Computational Geometry

Folding algorithms are a powerful tool in computational geometry, allowing us to solve complex geometric problems in a discrete and efficient manner. They are particularly useful in the context of polyhedra, where they can be used to construct and manipulate polyhedra in a discrete and computable manner.

#### 5.4a.4 Folding Algorithms and Linkages

## Chapter: Geometric Folding Algorithms: Linkages, Origami, Polyhedra

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the fascinating world of geometric folding algorithms. These algorithms are used to create complex structures and shapes by folding a flat sheet of paper or other material. This technique has been used for centuries in origami, the Japanese art of paper folding, and has recently gained popularity in the field of computational geometry.

Geometric folding algorithms are based on the principles of linkages, where a series of connected links are used to create a desired shape. These algorithms use mathematical equations and geometric transformations to determine the folding sequence and the resulting shape. They are particularly useful in creating polyhedra, which are three-dimensional shapes with flat faces.

In this chapter, we will cover the basics of geometric folding algorithms, including the concept of linkages and how they are used to create complex shapes. We will also explore the different types of polyhedra that can be created using these algorithms, and how they can be used in various applications.

## Chapter 6: Geometric Folding Algorithms: Linkages, Origami, Polyhedra




### Subsection: 5.4b Analysis Techniques

In this section, we will explore some of the analysis techniques used in discrete differential geometry and how they apply to folding algorithms. These techniques are essential for understanding the properties and behavior of geometric objects when they are folded.

#### 5.4b.1 Geometric Analysis

Geometric analysis is a fundamental technique in discrete differential geometry. It involves studying the geometric properties of a folded object, such as its shape, size, and orientation. This can be done using various mathematical tools, such as vectors, matrices, and transformations.

For example, consider a polyhedron that is folded along a set of lines. We can represent the polyhedron as a set of vertices, edges, and faces. By applying geometric analysis, we can determine the angles between the faces, the lengths of the edges, and the positions of the vertices. This information can then be used to understand the overall shape and behavior of the polyhedron when it is folded.

#### 5.4b.2 Topological Analysis

Topological analysis is another important technique in discrete differential geometry. It involves studying the topological properties of a folded object, such as its connectivity and orientation. This can be done using various topological tools, such as graphs, trees, and manifolds.

For example, consider a linkage that is folded into a complex shape. We can represent the linkage as a graph, where the vertices represent the joints and the edges represent the links. By applying topological analysis, we can determine the number of connected components, the number of loops, and the orientation of the linkage. This information can then be used to understand the overall connectivity and orientation of the linkage when it is folded.

#### 5.4b.3 Computational Analysis

Computational analysis is a powerful technique in discrete differential geometry. It involves using computer algorithms to analyze and manipulate geometric objects. This can be done using various computational tools, such as numerical methods, optimization algorithms, and machine learning techniques.

For example, consider a polyhedron that is folded along a set of lines. We can use a computational algorithm to determine the optimal folding pattern that minimizes the surface area of the polyhedron. This can be done by formulating the problem as an optimization problem and using numerical methods to find the optimal solution.

In conclusion, discrete differential geometry provides a powerful framework for analyzing and understanding the properties and behavior of geometric objects when they are folded. By using techniques such as geometric analysis, topological analysis, and computational analysis, we can gain a deeper understanding of the complex world of folding algorithms.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of geometric folding algorithms, linkages, and polyhedra. We have seen how these algorithms can be used to create intricate and beautiful structures, and how they have applications in various fields such as architecture, engineering, and art.

We then delved into the world of linkages, exploring how they can be used to create complex and dynamic structures. We learned about the different types of linkages, such as the four-bar linkage and the six-bar linkage, and how they can be used to create a variety of shapes and movements.

We learned about the different types of polyhedra, such as the cube and the dodecahedron, and how they can be created using geometric folding algorithms. ## Chapter: Geometric Folding Algorithms: Linkages, Origami, Polyhedra

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the fascinating world of geometric folding algorithms. These algorithms are used to create intricate and complex structures by folding and unfolding geometric shapes. This technique has been used in various fields such as architecture, engineering, and art. The beauty and simplicity of these algorithms have captured the attention of mathematicians, computer scientists, and designers alike.

We will begin by discussing the basics of geometric folding algorithms, including the concept of linkages and origami. Linkages are a series of connected lines or curves that can be folded and unfolded to create different shapes. Origami, on the other hand, is the art of paper folding, which has been practiced for centuries. We will explore how these two concepts are intertwined and how they are used in geometric folding algorithms.

Next, we will delve into the world of polyhedra, which are three-dimensional geometric shapes with flat faces. Polyhedra have been studied extensively in mathematics, and their properties have been used in various applications. ### Subsection: 5.4c Practical Applications

#### 5.4c.1 Folding Origami

Origami is a traditional Japanese art form that involves creating objects by folding paper. The process of folding origami is governed by a set of rules and principles, many of which can be understood using discrete differential geometry.

For example, consider the process of folding a simple origami crane. The crane is created by folding a square piece of paper along a set of lines. By applying geometric analysis, we can determine the angles and lengths of the folds, and use this information to create a set of instructions for folding the crane.

#### 5.4c.2 Folding Polyhedra

Polyhedra are three-dimensional objects that are bounded by flat polygonal surfaces. Folding polyhedra is a challenging problem that has been studied extensively in the field of discrete differential geometry.

For example, consider the process of folding a cube. The cube is created by folding a set of squares along a set of lines. By applying topological analysis, we can determine the number of connected components and the orientation of the cube, and use this information to create a set of instructions for folding the cube.

#### 5.4c.3 Folding Linkages

Linkages are mechanical systems that are composed of interconnected links. Folding linkages is a complex problem that has been studied extensively in the field of discrete differential geometry.

For example, consider the process of folding a kinematic chain, which is a type of linkage that is used in robotics. The kinematic chain is created by folding a set of links along a set of lines. By applying computational analysis, we can determine the number of connected components and the orientation of the kinematic chain, and use this information to create a set of instructions for folding the kinematic chain.

In conclusion, discrete differential geometry provides a powerful set of tools for understanding and manipulating geometric objects when they are folded. These tools have a wide range of practical applications, from creating origami cranes to designing complex mechanical systems.

### Subsection: 5.5a Basic Concepts

In this section, we will introduce some basic concepts in topology that are essential for understanding the process of folding. These concepts include topological spaces, manifolds, and homeomorphisms.

#### 5.5a.1 Topological Spaces

A topological space is a mathematical structure that describes the properties of a set and its subsets. It is defined by a set of points and a set of open sets, which are collections of points that satisfy certain properties. The open sets can be thought of as "neighborhoods" of points, and they allow us to define concepts such as continuity and convergence.

In the context of folding, topological spaces are used to describe the structure of a folded object. For example, a folded piece of paper can be represented as a topological space, where the open sets are the regions of the paper that are not covered by the folds.

#### 5.5a.2 Manifolds

A manifold is a topological space that locally resembles Euclidean space. In other words, every point in a manifold has a neighborhood that is homeomorphic to a Euclidean space. Manifolds are important in topology because they allow us to define concepts such as dimension and orientation.

In the context of folding, manifolds are used to describe the structure of a folded object. For example, a folded piece of paper can be represented as a manifold, where the neighborhoods of each point are the regions of the paper that are not covered by the folds.

#### 5.5a.3 Homeomorphisms

A homeomorphism is a continuous function that has a continuous inverse. In other words, a homeomorphism is a function that preserves the topological structure of a space. Homeomorphisms are important in topology because they allow us to identify different topological spaces as being "the same".

In the context of folding, homeomorphisms are used to describe the process of folding. For example, the process of folding a piece of paper can be represented as a homeomorphism, where the open sets of the folded paper are identified with the open sets of the unfolded paper.

In the next section, we will explore some practical applications of these basic concepts in topology.




### Subsection: 5.5b Analysis Techniques

In this section, we will explore some techniques for analyzing the topological structure of folded objects. These techniques include the use of topological invariants and the study of topological manifolds.

#### 5.5b.1 Topological Invariants

Topological invariants are properties of a topological space that remain unchanged under homeomorphisms. These properties are useful for classifying and identifying different topological spaces. In the context of folding, topological invariants can be used to classify different folded objects.

For example, the number of holes in a folded object is a topological invariant. If two folded objects have the same number of holes, then they are topologically equivalent, meaning that there exists a homeomorphism between them.

#### 5.5b.2 Topological Manifolds

As mentioned earlier, manifolds are important in topology because they allow us to define concepts such as dimension and orientation. In the context of folding, manifolds are used to describe the structure of a folded object.

For example, a folded piece of paper can be represented as a topological manifold, where the neighborhoods of each point are the regions of the paper that are not covered by the folds. By studying the topological structure of this manifold, we can gain insights into the properties of the folded object.

#### 5.5b.3 Computational Geometry Techniques

In addition to topological techniques, computational geometry techniques can also be used to analyze the topological structure of folded objects. These techniques involve using algorithms and computer simulations to study the properties of folded objects.

For example, the use of DPLL, a complete and efficient algorithm for solving the Boolean satisfiability problem, can be applied to the study of folded objects. By representing the topological structure of a folded object as a Boolean formula, we can use DPLL to determine the existence of a homeomorphism between two folded objects.

Furthermore, the use of the LLL algorithm, which is used for lattice basis reduction, can be applied to the study of folded objects. By representing the topological structure of a folded object as a lattice, we can use LLL to reduce the complexity of the problem and gain insights into the properties of the folded object.

In conclusion, the use of topological invariants, topological manifolds, and computational geometry techniques allows us to gain a deeper understanding of the topological structure of folded objects. By combining these techniques, we can develop powerful tools for analyzing and classifying different folded objects.

## Chapter: Chapter 6: Folding Algorithms and Origami

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of folding algorithms and origami. Folding algorithms are mathematical procedures that describe how to fold a piece of paper or other material into a specific shape. These algorithms are used in a variety of fields, including computer graphics, robotics, and architecture. Origami, on the other hand, is the traditional Japanese art of paper folding. It has been practiced for centuries and has evolved into a complex art form with its own vocabulary and techniques.

The combination of folding algorithms and origami is a powerful one. By using folding algorithms, origami artists can create intricate and complex designs that would be impossible to achieve by hand. This has led to the development of new techniques and styles in origami, pushing the boundaries of what is possible with paper folding.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles behind folding algorithms and how they are used in origami. We will also discuss the history and evolution of origami, as well as its cultural significance. Additionally, we will look at some of the most famous origami designs and how they were created using folding algorithms.

Whether you are a seasoned origami artist or a curious newcomer, this chapter will provide you with a deeper understanding of the art of paper folding and its connection to mathematics. So let's dive in and discover the beauty and complexity of folding algorithms and origami.




### Subsection: 5.6a Basic Concepts

In this section, we will introduce some basic concepts related to geometric constraints in folding. These concepts will provide a foundation for understanding the more complex algorithms and techniques discussed in the following sections.

#### 5.6a.1 Geometric Constraints

Geometric constraints are conditions that must be satisfied by the geometric objects involved in a folding process. These constraints can be of various types, such as topological constraints, dimensional constraints, and symmetry constraints.

Topological constraints refer to the relationships between the topological structures of the objects involved in the folding process. For example, in the folding of a polyhedron, the topological constraints would include the number of vertices, edges, and faces of the polyhedron.

Dimensional constraints refer to the dimensions of the objects involved in the folding process. For example, in the folding of a paper, the dimensional constraints would include the size and thickness of the paper.

Symmetry constraints refer to the symmetries of the objects involved in the folding process. For example, in the folding of a symmetric polyhedron, the symmetry constraints would include the axes of symmetry and the order of symmetry.

#### 5.6a.2 Folding Algorithms

Folding algorithms are computational procedures for performing a folding process. These algorithms take as input a set of geometric objects and a set of geometric constraints, and they produce as output a set of folded objects that satisfy the constraints.

Folding algorithms can be classified into two types: exact algorithms and heuristic algorithms. Exact algorithms guarantee that the output objects satisfy all the constraints, while heuristic algorithms aim to satisfy as many constraints as possible, but they do not guarantee that all the constraints will be satisfied.

#### 5.6a.3 Computational Geometry

Computational geometry is a field that deals with the geometric aspects of computational problems. In the context of folding, computational geometry is concerned with the representation, manipulation, and analysis of geometric objects.

Computational geometry provides the mathematical tools and techniques needed to formulate and solve folding problems. For example, it provides methods for representing geometric objects in a computer, algorithms for manipulating these objects, and theories for analyzing the results of the manipulations.

#### 5.6a.4 Geometric Folding Algorithms

Geometric folding algorithms are a specific type of folding algorithm that deals with the geometric aspects of folding. These algorithms are concerned with the geometric properties of the objects involved in the folding process, such as their size, shape, and symmetry.

Geometric folding algorithms use computational geometry techniques to solve folding problems. For example, they use geometric representations to represent the objects, geometric manipulations to perform the folding process, and geometric analyses to verify the results.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these concepts and explore how they are used in the design and implementation of folding algorithms.




### Subsection: 5.6b Constraint Handling Techniques

In the previous section, we introduced the basic concepts of geometric constraints in folding. In this section, we will delve deeper into the techniques used to handle these constraints in folding algorithms.

#### 5.6b.1 Constraint Satisfaction

Constraint satisfaction is a technique used to solve problems where the goal is to find a solution that satisfies a set of constraints. In the context of folding, constraint satisfaction involves finding a set of folded objects that satisfy all the geometric constraints.

Constraint satisfaction can be formulated as a mathematical optimization problem, where the goal is to minimize the number of constraints that are not satisfied. This can be represented as:

$$
\min \sum_{i=1}^{n} c_i
$$

where $c_i$ is a binary variable representing whether constraint $i$ is satisfied (0) or not (1).

#### 5.6b.2 Decomposition Method

The decomposition method is a constraint satisfaction technique that involves breaking down a problem into smaller subproblems that can be solved independently. In the context of folding, this can be used to break down a complex folding problem into simpler subproblems, making it easier to solve.

The decomposition method can be represented as a tree, where each node represents a subproblem and the edges represent the constraints between the subproblems. The goal is to find a solution that satisfies all the constraints at each node.

#### 5.6b.3 Implicit Data Structure

An implicit data structure is a data structure that is not explicitly defined, but can be constructed from other data. In the context of folding, this can be used to represent the geometric constraints in a more compact and efficient manner.

For example, consider a set of points in a plane. The implicit data structure for this set of points can be represented as a binary tree, where each node represents a point and the edges represent the distances between the points. This allows for efficient representation and manipulation of the points.

#### 5.6b.4 ECLiPSe

ECLiPSe is a constraint programming system that can be used to solve complex constraint satisfaction problems. It supports various constraint satisfaction techniques, including the decomposition method and implicit data structures.

ECLiPSe can be used to solve folding problems by representing the geometric constraints as constraints in the ECLiPSe language. The system then uses its constraint solver to find a solution that satisfies all the constraints.

#### 5.6b.5 Constraint Programming

Constraint programming is a technique used to solve problems by formulating them as constraint satisfaction problems. In the context of folding, this can be used to solve complex folding problems by formulating them as constraint satisfaction problems and using constraint programming techniques to find a solution.

Constraint programming can be represented as a mathematical optimization problem, where the goal is to find a solution that maximizes the number of constraints that are satisfied. This can be represented as:

$$
\max \sum_{i=1}^{n} c_i
$$

where $c_i$ is a binary variable representing whether constraint $i$ is satisfied (1) or not (0).

### Conclusion

In this section, we have explored various techniques for handling geometric constraints in folding algorithms. These techniques include constraint satisfaction, decomposition method, implicit data structure, ECLiPSe, and constraint programming. Each of these techniques has its own strengths and weaknesses, and the choice of which one to use depends on the specific requirements of the folding problem.




### Subsection: 5.6c Practical Applications

In this section, we will explore some practical applications of geometric constraints in folding. These applications demonstrate the versatility and usefulness of folding algorithms in various fields.

#### 5.6c.1 Origami Design

Origami, the Japanese art of paper folding, is a classic example of geometric folding. The goal of origami is to create a three-dimensional shape from a two-dimensional sheet of paper. This is achieved by folding the paper along specific lines, known as creases.

The design of origami models often involves solving a set of geometric constraints. For example, consider the design of a crane, a common origami model. The crane is composed of two legs, two wings, and a body. The legs and wings must be of equal length, and the wings must be perpendicular to the body. These constraints can be represented as a set of equations and inequalities, which can be solved using the techniques discussed in the previous section.

#### 5.6c.2 Polyhedron Construction

Polyhedra, three-dimensional shapes composed of flat polygons, are another important application of geometric folding. The construction of polyhedra often involves folding a piece of paper along specific lines to create the desired shape.

The construction of a polyhedron can be represented as a set of geometric constraints. For example, consider the construction of a cube. The cube is composed of six square faces, eight vertices, and twelve edges. The length of each edge must be equal, and the angles between adjacent edges must be 90 degrees. These constraints can be represented as a set of equations and inequalities, which can be solved using the techniques discussed in the previous section.

#### 5.6c.3 Robotics

Geometric folding also has applications in robotics. In particular, the design of robotic arms often involves solving a set of geometric constraints. The robotic arm must be able to reach a set of target points in space, while avoiding collisions with other objects. This can be represented as a set of constraints on the position and orientation of the arm, which can be solved using the techniques discussed in the previous section.

In conclusion, geometric constraints play a crucial role in the design and construction of various objects and structures. The techniques discussed in this chapter provide a powerful tool for solving these constraints and designing efficient and effective folding algorithms.

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of geometric folding algorithms, linkages, and polyhedra. We have seen how these algorithms can be used to create complex structures from simple geometric shapes, and how they can be applied in various fields such as origami, robotics, and architecture.

We have also delved into the mathematical foundations of these algorithms, understanding the principles of symmetry, duality, and transformation that underpin their operation. We have seen how these principles can be used to create elegant and efficient algorithms for folding and unfolding polyhedra, and how they can be extended to more complex structures such as linkages.

Finally, we have explored the intersection of these geometric folding algorithms with computational geometry, seeing how these algorithms can be implemented and optimized using modern computational techniques. We have seen how these techniques can be used to solve complex geometric problems, and how they can be used to create efficient and robust algorithms for folding and unfolding polyhedra and linkages.

In conclusion, geometric folding algorithms, linkages, and polyhedra offer a rich and fascinating field of study, with many opportunities for further exploration and research. Whether you are interested in the mathematical foundations, the practical applications, or the computational techniques, there is much to explore and discover in this exciting field.

## Chapter 6: Linkages, Origami, Polyhedra:




### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the various applications of geometric folding algorithms in the fields of linkages, origami, and polyhedra. These algorithms have been widely used in these areas due to their ability to create complex structures with precise folds.
